<div id="review_186647536" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/186647536" />
    <a title="Ivana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5838785-ivana"><img alt="Ivana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1393254397p2/5838785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/186647536?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ivana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ivana" href="/user/show/5838785-ivana">Ivana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788466304566" href="/book/show/62972.Conversaci_n_en_La_Catedral">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer186647536" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13016036289443215437">A book that changed the world, and it continues to do so. At first, I was concerned; I kept hearing just how difficult it is to read. To a certain extent, it was challenging because one sentence intertwines people, decades, events....but even so, once you get the "hang" of the narrative, you can't put it down. I think Mario Vargas Llosa achieved the something I believed wasn't possible- to summarize an entire history of Peruvian (and Latin American in general)society, its problems, turbulent pas</span>
  <span id="freeText13016036289443215437" style="display:none">A book that changed the world, and it continues to do so. At first, I was concerned; I kept hearing just how difficult it is to read. To a certain extent, it was challenging because one sentence intertwines people, decades, events....but even so, once you get the "hang" of the narrative, you can't put it down. I think Mario Vargas Llosa achieved the something I believed wasn't possible- to summarize an entire history of Peruvian (and Latin American in general)society, its problems, turbulent past, turbulent present and never-ending political challenges. </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7038907?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leftbanker" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leftbanker" href="/user/show/352711-leftbanker">Leftbanker</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788466304566" href="/book/show/62972.Conversaci_n_en_La_Catedral">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/352711-leftbanker?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/352711-leftbanker?shelf=read-in-spanish">read-in-spanish</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/352711-leftbanker?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/352711-leftbanker?shelf=spanish-literature">spanish-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7038907" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16928513515435863889">MVL was obviously heavily influenced by the stream-of-consciousness style of Faulkner. I prefer his work over Faulkner's any day and especially this novel. It has much more to say about society than the work of most writers. It's not just some sort of creative writing exercise, it is a portrait of Peruvian politics and culture from the era of Odría. Like some of his other early work, it is also a novel about Lima.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText8944098001341456075" style="display:none">I will not lie. It's not Mario Vargas Llosa's essential novel <b>The Time of the Hero</b>--the winner of the Nobel Prize, the literary inspiration that made me go to Peru. It is the adventure of my beloved reporter Tintin in <b>Prisoners of the Sun</b>. Ha-ha.<br /><br />The first time I read it when I was nine at the local library, Herge's comic had persuaded me to dream that someday I must go to the <b>Andes Mountain</b>. And now, I stand on the sacred hill to feel every bead of sweat and every ache of sunburn, to witness the sea of trees and admire the waves of the hills, and see the elusive ruined citadel <b>Machu Picchu</b>, I just feel that my life has been half fulfilled.<br /><br />Just as <b>Tintin in Tibet</b> that encouraged me to go to the Himalayas last year, Tintin in his spontaneous great adventure <b>Prisoners of the Sun</b>, risked his life to save his friends. Tintin and his companions were attacked by anacondas, wild condor, and alligators. Captain Haddock, Professor Calculus, Thomson &amp; Thompson are very funny, clumsy, and witty as always.<br /><br />Tintin &amp; Co. had visited many countries and only a few had visited by myself. That made me think, should I follow his journeys to visit all countries mentioned in the comics? Tintin also visited the moon in his most famous iconic adventure <b>Destination Moon</b>. Can I go there too? Well... Let's see. Ha-ha.<br /><br />I brought another great novel, perhaps the most well-known and the best book by Mario Vargas Llosa. Reading <b>Conversation in Cathedral</b> in his native country, gave me goosebumps and sent chills down my spine!<br /><br /><br />PS: Fun fact, in the comic, Tintin used coca (yep, that famous plant) to keep the cursed archaeologist in a coma. In the real-life Cuzco City (the big city near Machu Picchu), I was relieved to learn that the tea is not an illegal narcotic, it’s simply a traditional remedy that averts the headaches and breathlessness some visitors experience at high altitude.<br /><br /><br /> <a href="https://melquiadescaravan.wordpress.com/2019/10/02/andes-mountains-bag-1/" rel="nofollow noopener">Part 1</a></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1159241860" />
    <a title="John Gurney" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19356945-john-gurney"><img alt="John Gurney" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365779791p2/19356945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1159241860?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Gurney" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Gurney" href="/user/show/19356945-john-gurney">John Gurney</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1159241860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14209490990706336046">A chance encounter at a dog pound brings two former acquaintances together. They go to a bar called the Cathedral where Santiago, son of a once rich and powerful government minister, and Ambrosio, his father's former chauffeur, talk. Santiago wants to know what role his father had in the murder of his former mistress, a one-time Lima showgirl with shadowy connections. This 1969 book is rightly famous because, though the flashback Point-of-View narrative style takes some getting used to, it is a </span>
  <span id="freeText14209490990706336046" style="display:none">A chance encounter at a dog pound brings two former acquaintances together. They go to a bar called the Cathedral where Santiago, son of a once rich and powerful government minister, and Ambrosio, his father's former chauffeur, talk. Santiago wants to know what role his father had in the murder of his former mistress, a one-time Lima showgirl with shadowy connections. This 1969 book is rightly famous because, though the flashback Point-of-View narrative style takes some getting used to, it is a complex and gripping tale of sex, power, love, intrigue and corruption during the Odria dictatorship in 1950's Peru.<br /><br />This book is mostly dialogue and Vargas-Llosa's characters feel incredibly real because their conversations are so true-to-life. This is a long story, but it moves at a rapid pace. <i>Conversation in the Cathedral</i> is enjoyable- sometimes funny, sometimes saddening- but always worth reading.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14209490990706336046" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16351935609068317008">absolutely impressed. <br/>i feel i need to brake this down into: how i tried, couldn't, wouldn't even want to pick it up again..basically hating the first 400pages,but then breaking through, to the "other side", from where all things seem clear, logical. <br/><br/>it is a difficult read. it's the unpolished Llosa, clearly an earlier attempt at his intricate story weaving style. <br/><br/>what does strike me: he tells you from the start, from the first moment a new character appears what is going to happen to him, and t</span>
  <span id="freeText16351935609068317008" style="display:none">absolutely impressed. <br />i feel i need to brake this down into: how i tried, couldn't, wouldn't even want to pick it up again..basically hating the first 400pages,but then breaking through, to the "other side", from where all things seem clear, logical. <br /><br />it is a difficult read. it's the unpolished Llosa, clearly an earlier attempt at his intricate story weaving style. <br /><br />what does strike me: he tells you from the start, from the first moment a new character appears what is going to happen to him, and then he makes you live with them for 500-600 pages. he makes you feel sad when they eventually disappear. they are all awful, yet good in their own struggles. i cannot find one negative character in the entire book. <br /><br />monumental work. towards the end, i didn't want the book to finish.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2306183183" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2306183183" />
    <a title="Tom Nixon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5890720-tom-nixon"><img alt="Tom Nixon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348114568p2/5890720.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2306183183?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tom Nixon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tom Nixon" href="/user/show/5890720-tom-nixon">Tom Nixon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2306183183" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3885199446098666338">At this point, I don't really know what else I can say about Mario Vargas Llosa. Every book of his I've ever read has been powerful, incredible and straight up amazing. It's been awhile since I've read any Gabriel Garcia Marquez, Jorge Amado or Isabel Allende- and I still need to sit down and read more of Jorge Luis Borges- but Vargas Llosa belongs with the best of them- if for no other reason than his novels make you learn things you didn't necessarily know before. (Especially about the history</span>
  <span id="freeText3885199446098666338" style="display:none">At this point, I don't really know what else I can say about Mario Vargas Llosa. Every book of his I've ever read has been powerful, incredible and straight up amazing. It's been awhile since I've read any Gabriel Garcia Marquez, Jorge Amado or Isabel Allende- and I still need to sit down and read more of Jorge Luis Borges- but Vargas Llosa belongs with the best of them- if for no other reason than his novels make you learn things you didn't necessarily know before. (Especially about the history of Latin America.)<br /><br /><br /><br />Another thought I've had: I wonder what his novels would be like in Spanish? I might actually have to sit down and attempt one of his books in his native language just to try and see if his writing style comes across in a different way.<br /><br /><br /><br />So: Conversation In The Cathedral. Set in 1950s Peru, this book rather elegantly states the question that the characters ponder throughout the course of the book: "At what precise moment had Peru fucked itself up?" The two main characters are Santiago and Ambrosio, who have a chance meeting in Lima one day when Santiago is chasing a story and he finds his father's former Chauffeur, Ambrosio, working for the dog pound. Heading over to a local water hole known as The Cathedral, the two of them have a few drinks and catch up, each of them pondering what had gone wrong with their lives.<br /><br /><br /><br />Santiago is the black sheep of a rich family, working at a tabloid newspaper and rejecting his families wealth. In University, he had flirted with the Communist Party- mainly because he was interested in a fellow classmate of his, Aida- but after a student strike/uprising is put down by the regime, his interest in radical politics wanes and is soon abandoned. His father is a Senator and regime ally, Don Fermin who longs for Santiago to stop his rebellion and come back to go to law school so he can take over the family business- but it's his father's potential involvement in the murder of a notorious prostitute and mistress of a powerful regime ally that really sparks the story to life, especially when it comes to Ambrosio.<br /><br /><br /><br />Ambrosio, as it turns out knows more about the murder than he wants to admit and he tells Santiago his story and how his life more or less lead him first away from Lima, with a wife and a child and then back to Lima without them after his wife died and he more or less abandons his child/leaves her with their land lady out in the country. <br /><br /><br /><br />If the disillusionment both men feel at the state of their country wasn't the point of the book, the ending would feel somewhat unsatisfying- but in as it's set in 1950s Peru, a period of political and societal stagnation and malaise (at least that's what I took away from this book- I'm not finding much out in the interwebs that would disagree with that assessment) the disillusionment is the point. The ending works. It's melancholy and not particularly happy, but it fits. Life can be nothing but a series of unfortunate tragedies many of which can mean next to nothing at all for a lot of people. It's far too easy to be disillusioned by everything. <br /><br /><br /><br />Vargas Llosa's style is somewhat difficult to wrap your head around at first. It seems very jumbled at first- with dialogue shifting from one character to another one conversation to another, but the deeper you get into the book, clarity emerges and you begin to see the stories of Santiago and Ambrosio swing into focus. His style is sort of what makes me wonder about how these novels read in Spanish- it'd be interesting to see if there are nuances and aspects to his style that are different in his native language than in English- it sure seems like that would be the case, just because of translation, but how much is lost in translation would be interesting to find out too. <br /><br /><br /><br />Overall: a melancholy and powerful glimpse into the history of Peru, Mario Vargas Llosa proves yet again why he's one of the best writers in the world. I'd probably slot Conversations In The Cathedral in between Feast of the Goat and The War of The End of The World-- the former was more accessible and the latter considerably more dense, though both were amazing books. His style takes some getting used too, but what's nice about this book is that the more you read, the clearer everything becomes- it's like lifting a curtain, slowly. My Grade: **** out of ****</span>
  <a data-text-id="3885199446098666338" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_583932913" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/583932913" />
    <a title="Cord" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3655101-cord"><img alt="Cord" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/583932913?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cord" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cord" href="/user/show/3655101-cord">Cord</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789707702127" href="/book/show/12854444-conversaci-n-en-la-catedral">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer583932913&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer583932913" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5576127719754863485">I preface this by saying that Conversation in the Cathedral is the first novel that I have read in Spanish. Initially an exercise to improve my language comprehension, this provided a glimpse into the dynamic between Ambrosio and Santiago, the pair in dialogue. While I have only skimmed the English translation, it appears that Ambrosio’s use of the more formal usted when addressing Santiago is lost. This usted illustrates both Ambrosio’s self-esteem and his esteem for the Zavala family, consider</span>
  <span id="freeText5576127719754863485" style="display:none">I preface this by saying that Conversation in the Cathedral is the first novel that I have read in Spanish. Initially an exercise to improve my language comprehension, this provided a glimpse into the dynamic between Ambrosio and Santiago, the pair in dialogue. While I have only skimmed the English translation, it appears that Ambrosio’s use of the more formal usted when addressing Santiago is lost. This usted illustrates both Ambrosio’s self-esteem and his esteem for the Zavala family, considering that Santiago had retreated so far from his previous social station by the time the titular conversation occurs. I’m sure I would have other quibbles with the translation if I sought them, but that isn’t to say that I understood the text with utter clarity. Peruvian slang is not my terrain, and I am still calibrating to the language. Recognizing this, I can accept that I may have missed some nuance. <br /><br />The novel’s epigraph argues that the true novelist examines the entire social fabric and that the novel is l'histoire privée of nations. Regarding the formation of the “true novelist,” the book depicts the Odría dictatorship’s corrupting influence across the country’s spectrum, involving politicians, military, henchmen, students, prostitutes, journalists, etc. Vargas Llosa’s ambition to reach that echelon of Flaubert and Tolstoy, while commendable, also produces bloat. As for the epigraph’s latter half, this is a story inside hallways and corridors. We witness the clandestine student meetings. We are privy to the backroom political machinations. Odría is omitted. The subject is not what has been seen but what has not. As for what lies beyond the walls, the story’s Peru is, as Santiago proclaims, fucked: its Lima seen in shades of brown and grey, its dismal Pucallpa scant in opportunity, its Chincha that gets smaller and flatter as time passes. The prose flows especially nimbly while Vargas Llosa expounds on his dilapidated homeland. If there is a chief strength, though, it is the structure, which hopscotches through time and constantly redefines how the narrative is told. Many have found this approach abrasive, which is understandable. However, this form ultimately answers the question posed at the outset: “¿En qué momento se había jodido el Perú?” <br /><br />While reading Conversation in the Cathedral, I had the good fortune of encountering Borges’s “A New Refutation of Time.” Written in 1944 and revised in 1946, the Argentine’s essay extends the notion of idealism to a temporal realm, avowing that “I deny the existence of one single time, in which all things are linked.” Although Borges backtracks his assertion in the revised essay’s conclusion, it is still interesting to contrast Vargas Llosa’s approach to time with that of his Boom compatriot. Borges provides the example of Captain Isidoro Suárez and Thomas De Quincey, whose “deeds were not contemporaneous (they are now), inasmuch as the two men died…knowing nothing of each other…Every instant is autonomous.” In the Peruvian novel, each episode informs and is informed by another, their proximity amplifying their impact and creating the nexus of moral privation. Ludovico and Hipólito batter a dissident, then Trifulcio asks Tomasa to show him his son, then coronel Espina tells Cayo he needs a house if he wants to continue working, then Ludovico brags about the torture to Ambrosio. These instances may not be immediately contemporary, but to argue their autonomy from one another would be mistaken. Thus, there was no discernible moment when Peru became fucked.*<br /><br />The pairing of Ambrosio and Santiago offers another compelling diagnosis of what ails the country. As mentioned earlier, Ambrosio’s consistent usted when interacting with Santiago confines the younger Zavala to an upper-class he is desperate to escape. The two are bound to their fathers’ examples, regardless of their attempts to maneuver elsewhere. Although El Chispas inherits the role Santiago abdicated, the younger Zavala, for all his struggles, still heeds close to his father’s example. Don Fermín wants Santiago to return to school and to repair their relationship, incentivizing him with money and promises of work. Santiago wants Ambrosio to divulge the nature of his relationship with don Fermín, incentivizing him with money and promises of work. Likewise, Ambrosio may attempt to create a stable, domestic space with Amalia, but he eventually follows his father’s template. The circumstances differ, yet he still abandons his child and is forced into itinerancy, and by novel’s end, he comes to understand Trifulcio. He recognizes everything and recognizes nothing in Chincha, reflected in the restaurant’s name change from “Mi Patria” to “Victoria.” Whose victory remains to be seen. These recursive models, then, betoken paralysis. The prospects seem bleak. Cayo may fall, but he returns to Peru and to his businesses without reprisal. The thugs become the police. La Queta returns to work for Ivonne. To return to Borges: “Our destiny…is terrifying because it is irreversible and iron-bound.”<br /><br />*These texts could be, and probably are, unrelated. However, I found it serendipitous that I happened upon them concurrently and needed to collect my thoughts on them somewhere. Hopefully, I return to them soon.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5576127719754863485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1721756699" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1721756699" />
    <a title="J Arthur G" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58437883-j-arthur-g"><img alt="J Arthur G" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1721756699?book_show_action=true">Aug 07, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J Arthur G" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J Arthur G" href="/user/show/58437883-j-arthur-g">J Arthur G</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1721756699" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11538232680224827161">‘At what precise moment had Peru fucked itself up? … Peru all fucked up, Carlitos all fucked up, everybody all fucked up. He thinks: there’s no solution.’<br/><br/>Conversation in the Cathedral surprised me, mostly because, though I don’t exactly know why, I wasn’t expecting too much from it. This was my first Vargas Llosa, and I only really read it because I was in Peru travelling and, having vaguely meant to read something of his for several years, found an English translation.<br/><br/>The book offers a multi-la</span>
  <span id="freeText11538232680224827161" style="display:none">‘At what precise moment had Peru fucked itself up? … Peru all fucked up, Carlitos all fucked up, everybody all fucked up. He thinks: there’s no solution.’<br /><br />Conversation in the Cathedral surprised me, mostly because, though I don’t exactly know why, I wasn’t expecting too much from it. This was my first Vargas Llosa, and I only really read it because I was in Peru travelling and, having vaguely meant to read something of his for several years, found an English translation.<br /><br />The book offers a multi-layered and richly detailed portrayal of Peruvian society and politics in the 1950s, during the dictatorship of general Odria. The dictator himself is notably absent: whilst we’re shown the interrelated lives, loves and fortunes of all those around him, from government ministers to hired thugs and the ordinary people in his country, never do we exactly ‘meet’ the man himself. In this way I think Vargas Llosa was suggesting a sort of void, an emptiness that the Peruvian system was built around. (Interestingly, whilst I was reading the book Peru was in the midst of a general election. Keiko Fujimori, who Vargas Llosa – now in his eighties – vehemently opposed, had been the huge favourite but eventually lost by a fraction to Pedro Pablo Kuczynski. Essentially she lost because she is distrusted by many due her father – Alberto Fujimori was president from 1990 to 2000. He is currently in prison for corruption and human rights violations. This could become much more or a tangent than I intended … My point is that I wonder how much has actually changed in Peruvian politics in the last 60 years. I’m certainly not an expert but, at the very least, the events relayed in the novel and the current situation provide a fascinating comparison. See the quote above, taken from the first page.)<br /><br />By the end I felt I truly knew several of the characters, from the disillusioned Zavalita (a sort of young Vargas Llosa. Interestingly, he is referred to by several different names throughout the novel: Santiago Zavala – who ‘is’ Vargas Llosa – is Zavalita, Skinny, Superbrain, and even, briefly, the Marxist Julian, as though VL is making a point about the nature of personal identity...?) to the often frustratingly cowardly Ambrosio, to the bewilderingly cold Cayo Bermudez. I constantly asked myself what exactly motivated these people. Cayo ‘shithead’ is a brilliantly complex character. What motivates him? Sometimes it seems that it could be power, wealth, notoriety, lust, simple hatred, but all of these ideas are at times apparently refuted. Neither sexual nor political power brings him much pleasure. It’s possible to see Zavalita and Cayo as opposite sides of the same coin: whilst, to put it mildly, the aimless Zavalita is likeable and the driven Cayo is not, neither is satisfied, both seem to be striving for something whose nature they themselves are unsure of.<br /><br />Vargas Llosa’s style and the structure of this novel at least is not always easy. Actually sometimes it can be deeply frustrating. Conversations between different people, and at different times, are interwoven, often at the sentence level. The narration drifts in and out and around characters. Perseverance is richly rewarded though, as the portraits and the society are built up layer by layer to form a stunning panorama. As soon as I read the final page, 601, I went back to the first chapter: things become joined together, a sort of cyclical structure, the story painstakingly pieced together. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11538232680224827161" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Si5OSVH/YSAhcubYiFOlcCtGge/ki2Fv3NAFqoaNM3oD1MLwAV03iPJP6vPK5pQp/ggaDy49tdcKdxHkgcv5ew==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

Hopscotch is the dazzling, freewheeling account of Oliveira\'s astonishing adventures.\n\nThe book is highly influenced by Henry Miller’s reckless and relentless search for truth in post-decadent Paris and Daisetz Teitaro Suzuki’s modal teachings on Zen Buddhism.\n\nCortázar\'s employment of interior monologue, punning, slang, and his use of different languages is reminiscent of Modernist writers like Joyce, although his main influences were Surrealism and the French New Novel, as well as the &quot;riffing&quot; aesthetic of jazz and New Wave Cinema.\n\nIn 1966, Gregory Rabassa won the first National Book Award to recognize the work of a translator, for his English-language edition of Hopscotch. Julio Cortázar was so pleased with Rabassa\'s translation of Hopscotch that he recommended the translator to Gabriel García Márquez when García Márquez was looking for someone to translate his novel En este alucinante drama de la vida interior, seres intrincados en la bestial búsqueda de comprensión ceden a la mentira, la hipocresía y los celos desmedidos hasta el crimen más inexplicable. Aventura amorosa, aventura onírica, aventura del ser que dan testimonio de un asesinato, de cierta memoria culpable y de una valiente introspección. \nTécnicamente perfecta y de lectura apasionante, The son of a wandering country lawyer, he is brought up by Indian servants until he enters a Catholic boarding school at age 14. In this urban Spanish environment he is a misfit and a loner. The conflict of the Indian and the Spanish cultures is acted out within him as it was in the life of Arguedas. For the boy Ernesto, salvation is his world of dreams and memories. This chorus includes the muses of visceral realism, the beautiful Font sisters; their father, an architect interned in a Mexico City asylum; a sensitive young follower of Octavio Paz; a foul-mouthed American graduate student; a French girl with a taste for the Marquis de Sade; the great-granddaughter of Leon Trotsky; a Chilean stowaway with a mystical gift for numbers; the anorexic heiress to a Mexican underwear empire; an Argentinian photojournalist in Angola; and assorted hangers-on, detractors, critics, lovers, employers, vagabonds, real-life literary figures, and random acquaintances.\n\nA polymathic descendant of Borges and Pynchon, Roberto Bolaño traces the hidden connection between literature and violence in a world where national boundaries are fluid and death lurks in the shadow of the avant-garde. El fiscal Félix Chalcatana se ve envuelto en la investigación del primero de una serie de sanguinarios asesinatos. En un Perú amenazado por la guerrilla y los avances militares propios de una afianzada política dictatorial, para el fiscal la línea de investigación apunta hacia un solo camino: Sendero Luminoso. Al ritmo de un intenso thriller, el fiscal va perdiendo la poca inocencia que le quedaba al percatarse de los horrores que puede cometer el ejército y la policía de su país. A lo largo de su investigación, Félix sostiene conversaciones con testigos y elementos clave que, tras haber hablado con él, mueren torturados y asesinados.<\/span>\n  <a       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa">About Mario Vargas Llosa</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1569483424p3/22515.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/22515.Mario_Vargas_Llosa">Mario Vargas Llosa
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
6,205
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer629153242515356667">Mario Vargas Llosa, born in Peru in 1936, is the author of some of the most significant writing to come out of South America in the past fifty years. His novels include <i>The Green House</i>, about a brothel in a Peruvian town that brings together the innocent and the corrupt; <i>The Feast of the Goat</i>, a vivid re-creation of the Dominican Republic during the final days of General Rafael Trujillo’s insidiou</span>
  <span id="freeText629153242515356667" style="display:none">Mario Vargas Llosa, born in Peru in 1936, is the author of some of the most significant writing to come out of South America in the past fifty years. His novels include <i>The Green House</i>, about a brothel in a Peruvian town that brings together the innocent and the corrupt; <i>The Feast of the Goat</i>, a vivid re-creation of the Dominican Republic during the final days of General Rafael Trujillo’s insidious regime; and <i>Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter</i>, a comedic semi-autobiographical account of an aspiring writer named Marito Varguitas, who falls in love with Julia, the divorced sister-in-law of his Uncle Lucho. <br /><br />He is also a widely read and respected essayist, writing everything from newspaper opinion pieces to critical works on other writers, including <i>The Perpetual Orgy</i> on Flaubert.<br /><br />Vargas Llosa is also active outside the literary arena, and was a serious contender for the presidency of Peru in 1990 (eventually losing to the now disgraced Alberto Fujimori), an experience he documented in his memoir, <i>A Fish in the Water</i>. <br /><br />On the controversial nature of some of his work he said, “The writer’s job is to write with rigor, with commitment, to defend what they believe with all the talent they have. I think that’s part of the moral obligation of a writer, which cannot be only purely artistic. I think a writer has some kind of responsibility at least to participate in the civic debate. I think literature is impoverished, if it becomes cut from the main agenda of people, of society, of life.” <br /><br />He was awarded the Nobel Prize in Literature for the year 2010, "for his cartography of structures of power &amp; his trenchant images of the individual's resistance, revolt, and defeat".<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://us.macmillan.com/author/mariovargasllosa">http://us.macmillan.com/author/mariov...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="629153242515356667" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
